    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({

  <span id="freeText2463698474830260333" style="display:none">When it appeared in 1924, this work launched into the international spotlight a young and unknown poet whose writings would ignite a generation. W. S. Merwin's incomparable translation faces the original Spanish text. Now in a black-spine Classics edition with an introduction by Cristina Garcia, this book stands as an essential collection that continues to inspire lovers and poets around the world.<br />The most popular work by Chile's Nobel Prize-winning poet, and the subject of Pablo Larraín's acclaimed feature film Neruda starring Gael García Bernal.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer16060500486366515485">Tonight I Can Write <br/>Tonight I can write the saddest lines. <br/>Write, for example, "The night is starry <br/>and the stars are blue and shiver in the distance." <br/>The night wind revolves in the sky and sings. <br/>Tonight I can write the saddest lines. <br/>I loved her, and sometimes she loved me too. <br/>Through nights like this one I held her in my arms. <br/>I kissed her again and again under the endless sky. <br/>She loved me, sometimes I loved her too. <br/>How could one not have loved her great still eyes. <br/>Tonight I can write th</span>
  <span id="freeText16060500486366515485" style="display:none">Tonight I Can Write <br />Tonight I can write the saddest lines. <br />Write, for example, "The night is starry <br />and the stars are blue and shiver in the distance." <br />The night wind revolves in the sky and sings. <br />Tonight I can write the saddest lines. <br />I loved her, and sometimes she loved me too. <br />Through nights like this one I held her in my arms. <br />I kissed her again and again under the endless sky. <br />She loved me, sometimes I loved her too. <br />How could one not have loved her great still eyes. <br />Tonight I can write the saddest lines. <br />To think that I do not have her. To feel that I have lost her. <br />To hear the immense night, still more immense without her. <br />And the verse falls to the soul like dew to the pasture. <br />What does it matter that my love could not keep her. <br />The night is starry and she is not with me. <br />This is all. In the distance someone is singing. In the distance. <br />My soul is not satisfied that it has lost her. <br />My sight tries to find her as though to bring her closer. <br />My heart looks for her, and she is not with me. <br />The same night whitening the same trees. <br />We, of that time, are no longer the same. <br />I no longer love her, that's certain, but how I loved her. <br />My voice tried to find the wind to touch her hearing. <br />Another's. She will be another's. As she was before my kisses. <br />Her voice, her bright body. Her infinite eyes. <br />I no longer love her, that's certain, but maybe I love her. <br />Love is so short, forgetting is so long. <br />Because through nights like this one I held her in my arms <br />my soul is not satisfied that it has lost her. <br />Though this is the last pain that she makes me suffer <br />and these the last verses that I write for her. <br /></span>

<div id="review_179319418" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/179319418" />
    <a title="Richard" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3950572-richard"><img alt="Richard" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1302287787p2/3950572.jpg" /></a>

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/179319418?book_show_action=true">Jun 24, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Richard" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Richard" href="/user/show/3950572-richard">Richard</a>

        rated it

              <span id="reviewTextContainer179319418" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer7597383501872582507">3 THINGS ABOUT THIS BOOK<br/><br/>1. I went to Pablo Neruda's house once. Well, I went to one of his houses. He had three of them. I was teaching English in Santiago, Chile at the time. I went to Neruda's house in Valparaiso, which is a beach town. Weirdly enough, I visited on my twentieth birthday, on a lark, because I just happened to be vacationing in a nearby cabin with my host family.<br/>The thing that I remember about Pablo Neruda's house is that it's set back in a grove of dark pine trees and that ther</span>
  <span id="freeText7597383501872582507" style="display:none">3 THINGS ABOUT THIS BOOK<br /><br />1. I went to Pablo Neruda's house once. Well, I went to one of his houses. He had three of them. I was teaching English in Santiago, Chile at the time. I went to Neruda's house in Valparaiso, which is a beach town. Weirdly enough, I visited on my twentieth birthday, on a lark, because I just happened to be vacationing in a nearby cabin with my host family.<br />The thing that I remember about Pablo Neruda's house is that it's set back in a grove of dark pine trees and that there's sand everywhere. The sky was dark that day and it was cold, even though it was in the summer. <br />What I remember most about the experience wasn't the house itself, or the tour, or the nationalistic trinkets that vendors were trying to sell, but rather the feeling that the pine trees around the house evoked. They were like a dark magic that still sits in my mind six years later. Curious. Because this is the thing that stands out to me most about Neruda's poetry: the magnetic feeling of nature. The dirt and the flesh and the elements and the cold, wet, hot, dry. His poetry is so sensual, so primal, so tied to the earth (I know I sound like a hippie, but its <i>true</i>). When I look at my journal entries from this period in my life they're full of this sort of talk. I wrote about stars and cloud formations and the consistency of mud and the shape of a cheekbone. Southern Chile does this to you. The land casts a spell on you. Neruda put this spell into words. <br /><i>"Who writes your name in letters of smoke among the stars of the south? </i> <br /> <br /><br />2. I read "Twenty Love Poems" about five years ago, but I thought it was corny at the time. The edition I read had all these terrible erotic etchings in it. I hate that. I almost threw up. I don't believe in illustration much, because it insults the reader's imagination. Especially illustration in poetry, a genre which usually uses abstract images.<br />This time when I read "Twenty Love Poems" I read it slowly. And it reminded me of southern Chile. It reminded me of gloomy mountains, and the beauty of the rivers and clouds and the darkness of the ocean. It reminded me of that period of time, when I turned twenty, right before my life changed in many ways. <br />This time when I read "Twenty Love Poems" it meant something to me, because now I have been in love. I have been in love and have experienced all of the sorrows and thrills of love. Mostly sorrows. But the hope of future thrills.<br /><br />3. I found a musty Time/Life book about South America at a thrift store near my house. In the book there is a photograph of Mr. Neruda seated at a wooden desk at his house in Valparaiso. He is wearing a sweater and staring out the window. He has a pen and ink in front of him and he is holding his head as though he's deep in thought or distressed. Or both. I have hung this picture up in my apartment. It makes me want to write. It makes me remember all of the dark clouds. It makes me remember that <i>"love is so short, forgetting is so long."</i> <br /> </span>
  <a data-text-id="7597383501872582507" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1896793646" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1896793646" />
    <a title="Steven Godin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/26706841-steven-godin"><img alt="Steven Godin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605181037p2/26706841.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1896793646?book_show_action=true">Mar 25, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Steven Godin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Steven Godin" href="/user/show/26706841-steven-godin">Steven Godin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26706841-steven-godin?shelf=nobel-laureates">nobel-laureates</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26706841-steven-godin?shelf=poetry">poetry</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26706841-steven-godin?shelf=favourites">favourites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26706841-steven-godin?shelf=latin-america">latin-america</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1896793646" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16291443247196527512">Sensual poetic beauty, with a lingering sadness, this collection of poems written when Chilean Neruda was only 19 is a remarkable feat, but was not received well for the intense and sexual content, this time being 1924 I can understand why, however, there is no explicit text it's more to do with imagery using the surrounding environment, charting oceanic movements of passion along with the changing weather, to tell of youthful love. " I have gone marking the atlas of your body / with crosses of </span>
  <span id="freeText16291443247196527512" style="display:none">Sensual poetic beauty, with a lingering sadness, this collection of poems written when Chilean Neruda was only 19 is a remarkable feat, but was not received well for the intense and sexual content, this time being 1924 I can understand why, however, there is no explicit text it's more to do with imagery using the surrounding environment, charting oceanic movements of passion along with the changing weather, to tell of youthful love. " I have gone marking the atlas of your body / with crosses of fire. / My mouth went across: a spider, trying to hide. / In you, behind you, timid, driven by thirst.''. Becoming Neruda's best-loved work selling two million copies by the 1960s. Why? the imagery he conjures up is simply breathtaking but also painfully sad. ``On all sides I see your waist of fog, / and your silence hunts down my afflicted hours; / my kisses anchor, and my moist desire nests / in you with your arms of transparent stone.'' As irresistible as the sea, love is engulfing (``You swallowed everything, like distance. / . . . In you everything sank!''), but also departs as mysteriously as it arrived, leaving the poet's heart a ``pit of debris, fierce cave of the shipwrecked.''<br /><br />In terms of the intensity of romance and the tenderness of love, this collection encapsulates so much, each piece stands alone, but always remains close to the others. <br />Of the 20 poems on offer, not all made sense to me on first reading, but at only 70 pages in length, I will certainly be re-visiting in time. And then there's the seething "Song of Despair", a breakup song if I ever heard one, this for me was the highlight, words of such searing torment that were expressed with a heartbroken urgency. At such a young age, Neruda paints a mature picture of the abstract representations of life. To the contrary, the poems represent an open curiosity for different dimensions of life like sexuality, solitude, melancholy, and loss. Also, he does not idealize beauty and love, making his poetry far more authentically realistic. Nature is a constant presence throughout, with stars, rivers, wind, sky and sea reappearing in different contexts, lovers become nature itself. You can truly feel that each poem is reaching out to the other, sharing the same pleasure and plight.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="16291443247196527512" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_645456043" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/645456043" />
    <a title="Ahmad Sharabiani" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/614778-ahmad-sharabiani"><img alt="Ahmad Sharabiani" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1194930432p2/614778.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer645456043" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer5256547828101707198">Veinte Poemas de Amor y una Canción Desesperada = Twenty love Poems and a Song of Despair, Pablo Neruda <br/><br/>Twenty Love Poems and a Song of Despair, is a collection of romantic poems, by the Chilean poet Pablo Neruda, first published in 1924 by Editorial Nascimento of Santiago, when Neruda was 19. It was Neruda's second published work, after Twilight (1923) and made his name as a poet. <br/><br/>Twenty Love Poems and a Song of Despair was controversial for its eroticism, especially considering its author's ve</span>
  <span id="freeText5256547828101707198" style="display:none">Veinte Poemas de Amor y una Canción Desesperada = Twenty love Poems and a Song of Despair, Pablo Neruda <br /><br />Twenty Love Poems and a Song of Despair, is a collection of romantic poems, by the Chilean poet Pablo Neruda, first published in 1924 by Editorial Nascimento of Santiago, when Neruda was 19. It was Neruda's second published work, after Twilight (1923) and made his name as a poet. <br /><br />Twenty Love Poems and a Song of Despair was controversial for its eroticism, especially considering its author's very young age. Over the decades, Twenty poems has become Neruda's best-known work, and has sold more than 20 million copies. <br /><br /><b>Saddest Poem</b><br /><br />I can write the saddest poem of all tonight. <br /><br />Write, for instance: "The night is full of stars,<br />and the stars, blue, shiver in the distance." <br /><br />The night wind whirls in the sky and sings. <br /><br />I can write the saddest poem of all tonight.<br />I loved her, and sometimes she loved me too. <br /><br />On nights like this, I held her in my arms.<br />I kissed her so many times under the infinite sky. <br /><br />She loved me, sometimes I loved her.<br />How could I not have loved her large, still eyes? <br /><br />I can write the saddest poem of all tonight.<br />To think I don't have her. To feel that I've lost her. <br /><br />To hear the immense night, more immense without her.<br />And the poem falls to the soul as dew to grass. <br /><br />What does it matter that my love couldn't keep her.<br />The night is full of stars and she is not with me. <br /><br />That's all. Far away, someone sings. Far away.<br />My soul is lost without her. <br /><br />As if to bring her near, my eyes search for her.<br />My heart searches for her and she is not with me. <br /><br />The same night that whitens the same trees.<br />We, we who were, we are the same no longer. <br /><br />I no longer love her, true, but how much I loved her.<br />My voice searched the wind to touch her ear. <br /><br />Someone else's. She will be someone else's. As she once<br />belonged to my kisses.<br />Her voice, her light body. Her infinite eyes. <br /><br />I no longer love her, true, but perhaps I love her.<br />Love is so short and oblivion so long. <br /><br />Because on nights like this I held her in my arms,<br />my soul is lost without her. <br /><br />Although this may be the last pain she causes me,<br />and this may be the last poem I write for her. <br /><br />Pablo Neruda<br /><br />عنوانها: «ب‍ی‍س‍ت‌ غ‍زل‍واره‌ و ی‍ک‌ غ‍م‌آوا»؛ «بی‍س‍ت‌ ش‍ع‍ر ع‍اش‍ق‍ان‍ه‌ و ی‍ک‌ س‍رود ن‍وم‍ی‍دی‌»؛ «بیست شعر عاشقانه و آوایی از یاس»؛ شاعر: پابلو نرودا؛ تاریخ نخستین خوانش: سال 1974 میلادی<br /><br />عنوان: ب‍ی‍س‍ت‌ غ‍زل‍واره‌ و ی‍ک‌ غ‍م‌آوا؛ شاعر: پابلو نرودا؛ ت‍رج‍م‍ه‌ ب‍ه‌ ان‍گ‍ل‍ی‍س‍ی‌: دابل‍ی‍و.اس م‍روی‍ن‌؛ مترجم ک‍ری‍م‌ رش‍ی‍دی‍ان‌؛ اصفهان انتشارات بابک‏‫، ‌1351؛ در 85ص؛ موضوع ‏‫شعر شاعران شیلی به اسپانیایی‬ - سده 20م<br /><br />عنوان: ب‍ی‍س‍ت‌ ش‍ع‍ر ع‍اش‍ق‍ان‍ه‌ و ی‍ک‌ س‍رود ن‍وم‍ی‍دی‌؛ شاعر پ‍اب‍ل‍و ن‍رودا؛ برگردان ف‍رود خ‍س‍روان‍ی‌؛ تهران، امیرکبیر، 1352؛ در 71ص؛ چاپ دوم 1355؛<br /><br />‬عنوان: بیست شعر عاشقانه و آوایی از یاس؛ شاعر پابلو نرودا؛ مترجم هانیه نیکو؛ تهران تیسا‏‫، 1397؛ در 92ص؛ شابک 9786008942597؛‬<br /><br />پابلو نرودا نام مستعار «نفتالی ریکاردو الیسر ریه‌ س باسوآلتو»، سایستمدار و شاعر شیلیائی هستند.؛ ایشان نام «نرودا» را از روی نام «یان نرودا» نویسنده ی «چک» برگزیده بودند.؛ سپس «پابلو نرودا»، نام رسمی ایشان شد.؛ ایشان با چاپ همین کتاب با عنوان «بیست شعر عاشقانه و یک ترانه نومیدی»، به اوج شهرت رسیدند.؛<br /><br />نقل شعری از ایشان: «امشب میتوانم، غمگین ترین شعرها را بسرایم.؛ مثلا بنویسم: شب پرستاره است.؛ و ستاره ها آبی، چشمک زن، در دوردست؛ بادِ شبانه، در آسمان میچرخد، و آواز میخواند.؛ امشب میتوانم غمگین ترین شعرها را بسرایم.؛ او را دوست میداشتم، و گاه، او نیز، مرا دوست داشت.؛ در شبهایی اینچنین، او را در بر و بازوانم میگرفتم.؛ هماره زیر آسمان لایتناهی او را میبوسیدم.؛ او مرا دوست داشت، و گاه، من نیز او را دوست داشتم؛ چشمانِ آرام بزرگ ایشان را، چگونه میتوان دوست نداشت؟ امشب میتوانم غمگینترین شعرها را بسرایم.؛ فکر اینکه او را ندارم، احساس اینکه از دستش داده ام.؛ گوش دادن به شب بزرگوار، که بدون او بزرگتر هم هست.؛ و شعر، که نزول میکند، بر روحم، همانند شبنم، که بر علف.؛ چقدر اندوهگینم که عشقم نتوانست او را نگهدارد؟ شب پرستاره است، و او، با من نیست.؛ تا همین اندازه کافی است.؛ دوردست، یکی آواز میخواند.؛ دوردست؛ روحم بدون او گم شده، تا مگر او را نزدم بیاورد.؛ چشمانم دنبالش میگردد.؛ دلم او را میجوید.؛ و او با من نیست.؛ همان شبها، همان درختان را سپید میکنند.؛ ما از آن زمان، دیگر همان کس نیستیم که بودیم.؛ دیگر دوستش ندارم - درست است - اما؛ چقدر دوستش داشتم.؛ صدایم در جستجوی بادی ست، تا به گوشش برساند.؛ کسی دیگر، او کسی دیگر را میخواهد.؛ همچون پیش، که بوسه هایم را داشت.؛ صدایش، بدن روشنش، چشمان نامحدودش، دیگر دوستش ندارم –درست است-؛ اما شاید هم دوستش داشته باشم.؛ عشق اینچنین کوتاه، و فراموشی اینقدر طولانی؛ زیرا در شبهایی اینچنین، او را در آغوشم میگرفتم؛ روحم بدون او گم شده.؛ اگرچه شاید این آخرین رنجی باشد؛ که به خاطر او میکشم؛ و این آخرین شعری ست؛ که برایش میسرایم.؛ پایان نقل از پابلو نرودا؛<br /><br />تاریخ بهنگام رسانی 17/05/1399هجری خورشیدی؛ ا. شربیانی</span>
  <a data-text-id="5256547828101707198" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_1955884758" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1955884758" />
    <a title="Samra Yusuf" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/56939723-samra-yusuf"><img alt="Samra Yusuf" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1584090140p2/56939723.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1955884758?book_show_action=true">Apr 29, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Samra Yusuf" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Samra Yusuf" href="/user/show/56939723-samra-yusuf">Samra Yusuf</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/56939723-samra-yusuf?shelf=fav">fav</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1955884758" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13591900315900568437">How beautifully fragile we are, that so many things take but a moment to alter who we are, for forever. We are all, just an unforeseen encounter, an unexpected phone call, a diagnosis, a newly found love, or a broken heart away from becoming a completely different person. Our hearts betray us to the places we never thought be visiting, our reasons fail us to the most uninvited chasms we surrender ourselves into, knowingly. Our souls ripped open and raw, our hearts on display, Love leaves vulnera</span>
  <span id="freeText13591900315900568437" style="display:none">How beautifully fragile we are, that so many things take but a moment to alter who we are, for forever. We are all, just an unforeseen encounter, an unexpected phone call, a diagnosis, a newly found love, or a broken heart away from becoming a completely different person. Our hearts betray us to the places we never thought be visiting, our reasons fail us to the most uninvited chasms we surrender ourselves into, knowingly. Our souls ripped open and raw, our hearts on display, Love leaves vulnerable at places, we never thought be touched. Neruda, explores love in many forms and stages. He writes about love that have been lost, love that replace solitude, and love that haunt lovers forever. At last, in the Song of Despair he encapsulates many of the concerns established through the sequence and offers a heightened emotional culmination: It is the hour of departure. Oh abandoned one!<br /><i>In you the wars and the flights accumulated.<br />From you the wings of the song bird rose.<br />You swallowed everything, like distance.<br />Like the sea, like times. In you everything sank!</i><br />Love in Nerudian realms starts as the most intense of passions, the yet alone lover hastens to explore every pore, he aches to become one with the beloved, there’s nothing else but the yearning to be close to the other, the presence that is felt through a hand held, a voice heard, or a smile seen, leaves him battered with desire, as souls know no calendar, nor do they understand the time or distance, they strive to collide, to become one, even for a moment, that lives for eternity..<br /><i>I was alone like a tunnel. The birds fled from me,<br />and night swamped me with its crushing invasion.<br />To survive myself I forged you like a weapon,<br />like an arrow in my bow, a stone in my sling.<br />But the hour of vengeance falls, and I love you.<br />Body of skin, of moss, of eager and firm milk.<br />Oh the goblets of the breast! Oh the eyes of absence!<br />Oh the roses of the pubis! Oh your voice, slow and sad!</i><br />Lover is agitated to the point of uncertainty, the point where, we no longer are reader, but exchange roles, as if words are given to the choking thoughts we’ve long been weaving inside us, when I was reading them, I was filled with such longing and my heart sighed like it was in despair even when it wasn’t, or it truly was!<br /><i>I like for you to be still, and you seem far away.<br />It souds as though you were lamenting, a butterfly cooing like a dove.<br />And you hear me from far away, and my voice does not reach you:<br />Let me come to be still in your silence.<br />And let me talk to you with your silence<br />that is bright as a lamp, simple as a ring.<br />You are like the night, with its stillness and constellations.<br />Your silence is that of a star, as remote and candid.<br />I like for you to be still: it is as though you were absent,<br />distant and full of sorrow as though you had died.<br />One word then, one smile, is enough.<br />And I am happy, happy that it's not true.</i><br /><br />Sensual Passion thaws into melancholy and melancholy weds despair, and we sense the tone of lover vicissitudes when faced with departure!<br /><i>How terrible and brief was my desire of you!<br />How difficult and drunken, how tensed and avid.<br />Cemetery of kisses, there is still fire in your tombs,<br />still the fruited boughs burn, pecked at by birds.<br />Oh the bitten mouth, oh the kissed limbs,<br />oh the hungering teeth, oh the entwined bodies.<br />Oh the mad coupling of hope and force<br />in which we merged and despaired.<br />And the tenderness, light as water and as flour.<br />And the word scarcely begun on the lips.<br />This was my destiny and in it was the voyage of my longing,<br />and in it my longing fell, in you everything sank!</i><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="13591900315900568437" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2256920670" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2256920670" />
    <a title="Bill Kerwin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/83582-bill-kerwin"><img alt="Bill Kerwin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442940975p2/83582.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2256920670?book_show_action=true">Jan 20, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bill Kerwin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bill Kerwin" href="/user/show/83582-bill-kerwin">Bill Kerwin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780142437704" href="/book/show/399095.Twenty_Love_Poems_and_a_Song_of_Despair">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83582-bill-kerwin?shelf=spanish-lit">spanish-lit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83582-bill-kerwin?shelf=poetry">poetry</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2256920670" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6497865369093015793"><br/>Stephen Dobyns, in his forward to this edition, tells of what occurred at a poetry event in Venezuela, sometime in the ‘60’s. After Chilean poet Pablo Neruda concluded his prepared reading, he opened himself up to requests. The first request, from a member of this audience of six hundred, was for poem #20 from this book (“Tonight I could write the saddest lines”). When Neruda apologized, saying he had neglected to bring that particular poem, “<i>four hundred people stood up</i> and recited the poem to </span>
  <span id="freeText6497865369093015793" style="display:none"><br />Stephen Dobyns, in his forward to this edition, tells of what occurred at a poetry event in Venezuela, sometime in the ‘60’s. After Chilean poet Pablo Neruda concluded his prepared reading, he opened himself up to requests. The first request, from a member of this audience of six hundred, was for poem #20 from this book (“Tonight I could write the saddest lines”). When Neruda apologized, saying he had neglected to bring that particular poem, “<i>four hundred people stood up</i> and recited the poem to him.”<br /><br />For a man like me from the United States, such a story sounds almost fantastic, but then it is hard for a citizen of the good ol’ USA to imagine what its like to live in a country with such a passion for beautiful verse. But then, Spanish speakers do love their poetry, and this little book is one of the most popular of all time. Since Neruda published it in 1924 (when he was nineteen!), it has sold over 20 million copies. <br /><br />This book is justly famous for its eroticism, but it should be praised for the richness of its natural images too. The images of trees, streams, and animals of all kinds never seem forced or automatic, but rather seem to be part of an ancient and effortless vocabulary, as if either Nature herself had written these passionate lines, or she were the lover to be praised.<br /><br />This translation by W.S. Merwin—a distinquished poet in his own right—is the best known English version. It is simple, eloquent, and natural—as any good translation of this book must be.<br /><br />I love “Tonight I could write the saddest lines,” but I won’t reproduce it here. It is rather long, and, besides, it is the best known poem from the book. Instead, I’ll share with you two of its shorter poems that I like almost as much:<br /><br /><blockquote>
  <i>III: AH VASTNESS OF PINES<br /><br />Ah vastness of pines, murmur of waves breaking<br />slow play of lights, solitary bell,<br />twilight falling in your eyes, toy doll,<br />earth-shell, in whom the earth sings!<br /><br />In you the rivers sing and my soul flees in them<br />as you desire, and you send it where you will.<br />Aim my road on your bow of hope<br />and in a frenzy I will free my flock of arrows<br /><br />On all sides I see your waist of fog,<br />and your silence hunts down my afflicted hours;<br />my kisses anchor, and my moist desire nests<br />In you with your arms of transparent stone.<br /><br />Ah your mysterious voice that love tolls and darkens<br />in the resonant and dying evening!<br />Thus in deep hours have I seen, over the fields,<br />the ears of wheat tolling in the mouth of the wind.<br /><br /><br /><br />X: WE HAVE LOST EVEN<br /><br />We have lost even this twilight<br />No one saw us this evening hand in hand<br />whiole the blue night dropped on the world.<br /><br />I have seen from my window<br />the fiesta of sunset in the distant mountain tops.<br /><br />Sometimes a piece of sun<br />burned like a coin between my hands.<br /><br />I remembered you with my soul clenched<br />in that sadness of mine that you know.<br /><br />Where were you then?<br />Who else was there?<br />Saying what?<br />Why will the whole of love come on my suddenly<br />when I am sad and feel you are far away?<br /><br />The book fell that is always turned at twilight<br />and my cape rolled like a hurt dog at my feet.<br /><br />Always, always you recede through the evenings<br />towards where the twilight goes erasing statues.</i>
</blockquote></span>
  <a data-text-id="6497865369093015793" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer7100425031253788007">I do not love you except because I love you;<br/>I go from loving to not loving you,<br/>From waiting to not waiting for you<br/>My heart moves from cold to fire.<br/><br/>I love you only because it's you the one I love;<br/>I hate you deeply, and hating you<br/>Bend to you, and the measure of my changing love for you<br/>Is that I do not see you but love you blindly.<br/><br/>Maybe January light will consume<br/>My heart with its cruel<br/>Ray, stealing my key to true calm.<br/><br/>In this part of the story I am the one who<br/>Dies, the only one, and I will die of l</span>
  <span id="freeText7100425031253788007" style="display:none">I do not love you except because I love you;<br />I go from loving to not loving you,<br />From waiting to not waiting for you<br />My heart moves from cold to fire.<br /><br />I love you only because it's you the one I love;<br />I hate you deeply, and hating you<br />Bend to you, and the measure of my changing love for you<br />Is that I do not see you but love you blindly.<br /><br />Maybe January light will consume<br />My heart with its cruel<br />Ray, stealing my key to true calm.<br /><br />In this part of the story I am the one who<br />Dies, the only one, and I will die of love because I love you,<br />Because I love you, Love, in fire and blood.<br /><br /><br />worthy book for all the tragic romantikus outthere =P</span>

<div id="review_1727777730" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1727777730" />
    <a title="Gaurav" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8148922-gaurav"><img alt="Gaurav" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1507460267p2/8148922.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1727777730?book_show_action=true">May 17, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Gaurav" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Gaurav" href="/user/show/8148922-gaurav">Gaurav</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780224074414" href="/book/show/833091.Twenty_Love_Poems_and_a_Song_of_Despair">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8148922-gaurav?shelf=latin-american-beauty">latin-american-beauty</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8148922-gaurav?shelf=poetry">poetry</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1727777730" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13976843086607485393"><i>I no longer love her, that's certain, but maybe I love her. Love is so short, forgetting is so long.</i><br/> -Pablo Neruda<br/><br/><img src='https://images.gr-assets.com/photos/1526588835p8/3699643.jpg' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>Neruda was accomplished in a variety of styles ranging from erotically charged love poems like his collection Twenty Poems of Love and a Song of Despair, surrealist poems, historical epics, and overtly political manifestos. Twenty Love Poems and a Song of Despair is an amazing collection of poetry. His words caress the senses; imagery so delicious and fulfilling you can not only see </span>
  <span id="freeText13976843086607485393" style="display:none"><i>I no longer love her, that's certain, but maybe I love her. Love is so short, forgetting is so long.</i><br> -Pablo Neruda<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/photos/1526588835p8/3699643.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Neruda was accomplished in a variety of styles ranging from erotically charged love poems like his collection Twenty Poems of Love and a Song of Despair, surrealist poems, historical epics, and overtly political manifestos. Twenty Love Poems and a Song of Despair is an amazing collection of poetry. His words caress the senses; imagery so delicious and fulfilling you can not only see it but smell and taste and feel it, this is a great collection of passionate poetic imagery with a tinge of sadness but, sadly though, it was scandalized due to its sexual content which shows limited understanding of human beings in general.<br><br><br><br>Pablo Neruda brings love and rebellion to mind as soon as you think about him, he is considered to be synonym of love and strong emotions. Though I'm not a great fan of love poetry- I may have some preconceived notions- however I was spellbound and taken aback with pleasant surprise when I read Neruda. Time stops and modern life, with all its hustle and bustle, disappears. The weary reader, beaten to death by the speed at which today’s life is going, will be transported to a differently-paced world where time is not dictated by the rules of the clock but instead by the cadence of Neruda’s poetry. The city disappears and is replaced by mountains; the honking of cars is replaced by the singing of birds; and the indifference and cynicism that you feel will be replaced by a sense of longing. Such are the power of Neruda’s words. This is the world created by poetic artistry of Neruda.<br><br><i>
  <b>Here I Love You</b>
</i><br>Here I love you and the horizon hides you in vain.<br>I love you still among these cold things.<br>Sometimes my kisses go on those heavy vessels<br>that cross the sea towards no arrival.<br>I see myself forgotten like those old anchors.<br>The piers sadden when afternoon moors there.<br>My life grows tired, hungry to no purpose.<br>I love what I do not have. You are so far.<br>My loathing wrestles with the slow twilights.<br>But night comes and starts to sing to me,<br>The moon turns its clockwork dream.<br><br><i>
  <b>I like For You To Be Still</b>
</i><br>I like for you to be still: it is as though you were absent,<br>and you hear me from far away and my voice does not touch you.<br>It seems as though your eyes had flown away<br>and it seems that a kiss had sealed your mouth.<br><br><br><br>Neruda's ballads exemplify an enchanting surrender that invigorates the helplessness of new love and evacuates the disgrace out of the advances that are only a toll before the music. Love as we know it is a dangerous passion, it makes human beings vulnerable to be deceived, it brings with it anguish which keeps on haunting them till eternity, however some of the passions may not be as demanding as Neruda so aptly congeals the parts of nature with that of a human body. But even that innocuous seeming passion brings the feeling of despair, for these parts of nature reminds one of one's lover and the vulnerability associated with love encircles the person.<br><br><i>
  <b>So That You Will Hear me </b>
</i><br>The wind of anguish still hauls on them as usual.<br>Sometimes hurricanes of dreams still knock them over.<br>You listen to other voices in my painful voice.<br>Lament of old mouths, blood of old supplications.<br>Love me, companion. Don't forsake me. Follow me.<br>Follow me, companion, on this wave of anguish.<br><br><i>
  <b>Every Day You Play</b>
</i><br>Every day you play with the light of the universe.<br>Subtle visitor, you arrive in the flower and the water,<br>You are more than this white head that I hold tightly<br>as a cluster of fruit, every day, between my hands<br><br><br><br>The tone in these ballads is steady, through these poems you can feel that these lyrics are addressing each other, having a similar anguish and joy. Be that as it may, in The Song of Despair there is an obvious change in the tone, the speaker is edgy as the memory of a sweetheart frequents him. The symbolism in these ballads is of wreck and misfortune: pit of garbage, furious give in of the shipwreck and substance. He likewise rehashes the line In you everything six times and each time its significance changes as the ballad develops in passionate power and agony. Additionally this reiteration gives the sonnet a melodic quality that relates with his want to title the ballad a song.<br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/photos/1526588806p8/3699642.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><i>
  <b>A Song of Despair</b>
</i><br>The memory of you emerges from the night around me.<br>The river mingles its stubborn lament with the sea.<br><br>Deserted like the wharves at dawn.<br>It is the hour of departure, oh deserted one!<br>........<br>It is the hour of departure, the hard cold hour<br>which night fastens to all the timetables.<br><br>The rustling belt of the sea girdles the shore.<br>Cold stars heave up, black birds migrate.<br><br>Deserted like the wharves at dawn.<br>Only the tremulous shadow twists in my hands.<br><br>Oh farther than everything. Oh farther than everything.<br>It is the hour of departure. Oh abandanoed one!<br><br><br><br>It may look to a casual reader that these poems are about love between man and woman (the preconceived notions about the writer would also help) but it would be naive of a reader to think so, for the poems magnificently unwraps the anguish, uncertainty, longing and despair which are so elegantly weaved with the disguise of love.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13976843086607485393" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1846401817" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1846401817" />
    <a title="Seemita" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/34622220-seemita"><img alt="Seemita" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1473185997p2/34622220.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1846401817?book_show_action=true">Dec 21, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Seemita" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Seemita" href="/user/show/34622220-seemita">Seemita</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780224074414" href="/book/show/833091.Twenty_Love_Poems_and_a_Song_of_Despair">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/34622220-seemita?shelf=poetry">poetry</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/34622220-seemita?shelf=singing_words">singing_words</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/34622220-seemita?shelf=romance">romance</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/34622220-seemita?shelf=nobel-laureates">nobel-laureates</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/34622220-seemita?shelf=translated">translated</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/34622220-seemita?shelf=chile">chile</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1846401817" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10757275191047902738">Tempting as it may appear to wrap the poetic pearls from this collection of Neruda’s heartbeats into a warm shawl of erotic wool, do resist it and pause. <br/><br/>These loquacious verses that assemble at the nape of a lover or ripple playfully across the soft mountains of a beloved’s waist, magnify when viewed through the dual lenses of <i>
  <b>night</b>
</i> and <i>
  <b>water</b>
</i>.<blockquote>
  <i>I have said that you sang in the wind<br/>like pines and like masts.<br/>Like them you are tall and taciturn,<br/>and you are sad, all at once, like a voyage.</i></blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText10757275191047902738" style="display:none">Tempting as it may appear to wrap the poetic pearls from this collection of Neruda’s heartbeats into a warm shawl of erotic wool, do resist it and pause. <br /><br />These loquacious verses that assemble at the nape of a lover or ripple playfully across the soft mountains of a beloved’s waist, magnify when viewed through the dual lenses of <i>
  <b>night</b>
</i> and <i>
  <b>water</b>
</i>.<blockquote>
  <i>I have said that you sang in the wind<br />like pines and like masts.<br />Like them you are tall and taciturn,<br />and you are sad, all at once, like a voyage.<br /><br />You gather things to you like an old road.<br />You are peopled with echoes and nostalgic voices.<br />I awoke and at times birds fled and migrated<br />that had been sleeping in your soul.</i>
</blockquote> Throughout this collection, there are elements that sprout from these two shores, taking their own boundless attire once left to the ocean of the author’s imagination. I found it interesting to note that Neruda wrote these poems when he was just 19, implying the failures of his political aspirations and love relationships, besides his daughter’s premature death were still far away. Despite none of the later-years’ blackness charring his soul, his propensity to hinge his ode on <i>night</i> and <i>water</i> mirrors a certain yearning that isn’t a slave of reciprocity or longevity. Like the night and the nocturnal swagger, arousal is a reality and yet a mirage, something that will come in certainty but will be short-lived. Like the adaptability and slightness of water, love can superimpose rebuttals and tide over long leaps of unrequited love to reach a state where it will be nothing but itself, complete and calm. <br /><br />Neruda’s poems personify a charming surrender that fortifies the vulnerability of new love and removes the shame out of the advances that are nothing but a chime before the music. <i>
  <blockquote>In the moist night my garment of kisses trembles<br />charged to insanity with electric currents,<br />heroically divided into dreams<br />and intoxicating roses practicing on me.</blockquote>
</i> His hero gets high on the flowers and seasons, on the days and the night, on proximity and distance, on silence and chatter – his hero is the quintessential lover who refuses to let the flame of his emotion die, shielding it with verses after verses of untamable urgency. And with the final poem, one can almost imagine him slumping to the ground, dropping his gaze from his object of love and yet, not allowing the humming of his heart to lay still.<i>
  <blockquote>Cemetery of kisses, there is still fire in your tombs,<br />still the fruited boughs burn, pecked at by birds.</blockquote>
</i><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="10757275191047902738" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_971093072" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/971093072" />
    <a title="Florencia" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/21575078-florencia"><img alt="Florencia" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1601866797p2/21575078.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/971093072?book_show_action=true">Jun 18, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Florencia" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Florencia" href="/user/show/21575078-florencia">Florencia</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21575078-florencia?shelf=poetry">poetry</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer971093072" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2123399140383069088"><blockquote>Oír la noche inmensa, más inmensa sin ella.<br/>Y el verso cae al alma como al pasto el rocío. <br/><br/>*<br/><br/>To hear the immense night, still more immense without her.<br/>And the verse falls to the soul like dew to the pasture.</blockquote><br/>This is musicality being butchered. <br/>Always more interested in the song of despair, but I feel like giving this another try due to someone's review, and after many years.<br/><br/>April 24, 19<br/><br/>*<br/><br/>Sometimes, <i>the more things change, the more they stay the same.</i> Again, three stars. A bit tragic, despite being a</span>
  <span id="freeText2123399140383069088" style="display:none"><blockquote>Oír la noche inmensa, más inmensa sin ella.<br />Y el verso cae al alma como al pasto el rocío. <br /><br />*<br /><br />To hear the immense night, still more immense without her.<br />And the verse falls to the soul like dew to the pasture.</blockquote><br />This is musicality being butchered. <br />Always more interested in the song of despair, but I feel like giving this another try due to someone's review, and after many years.<br /><br />April 24, 19<br /><br />*<br /><br />Sometimes, <i>the more things change, the more they stay the same.</i> Again, three stars. A bit tragic, despite being able to appreciate - in a way I couldn't before - Neruda's lyricism and its natural voluptuousness, especially considering he wrote this collection when he was only 19. <br /><blockquote><b>Pensando, enredando sombras en la profunda soledad.<br />Tú también estás lejos, ah más lejos que nadie.<br />Pensando, soltando pájaros, desvaneciendo imágenes, <br />enterrando lámparas.<br /><br />*<br /><br />Thinking, tangling shadows in the deep solitude.<br />You are far away too, oh farther than anyone.<br />Thinking, freeing birds, dissolving images,<br />burying lamps.</b><br /><br />from Poem XVII</blockquote><br />The rest of the experience remains intact. But I sensed it. This is the kind of poetry I can relate to; the intensity and sentimentality I can bear:<br /><br /><b>Lo perdido</b><br />¿Dónde estará mi vida, la que pudo <br />haber sido y no fue, la venturosa <br />o la de triste horror, esa otra cosa <br />que pudo ser la espada o el escudo <br /><br />y que no fue? ¿Dónde estará el perdido <br />antepasado persa o el noruego, <br />dónde el azar de no quedarme ciego, <br />dónde el ancla y el mar, dónde el olvido <br /><br />de ser quien soy? ¿Dónde estará la pura <br />noche que al rudo labrador confía <br />el iletrado y laborioso día, <br /><br />según lo quiere la literatura? <br />Pienso también en esa compañera <br />que me esperaba, y que tal vez me espera.<br /><br />*<br /><br /><b>What is lost </b><br />I wonder where my life is, the one that could<br />have been and never was, the daring one<br />or the one of gloomy dread, that other thing<br />which could as well have been the sword or shield<br /><br />but never was? I wonder where is my lost<br />Persian or Norwegian ancestor,<br />where is the chance of my not being blind,<br />where is the anchor, the ocean, where the forgetting<br /><br />to be who I am? I wonder where the pure<br />night is that the unlettered working day<br />entrusts to the rough laborer so that he<br /><br />can also feel the love of literature<br />I also think about a certain companion<br />who waited for me once, perhaps still waits.<br /><br />Love poem by <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/500.Jorge_Luis_Borges" title="Jorge Luis Borges" rel="nofollow noopener">Jorge Luis Borges</a><br /><br /><br /><br />April 26, 19<br />* Later on <a href="https://nonbookreviews.wordpress.com/" rel="nofollow noopener">my blog</a>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2123399140383069088" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_1147252278" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1147252278" />
    <a title="Jibran" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5407952-jibran"><img alt="Jibran" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1572721231p2/5407952.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1147252278?book_show_action=true">Dec 30, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jibran" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jibran" href="/user/show/5407952-jibran">Jibran</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140186482" href="/book/show/911710.Twenty_Love_Poems_and_a_Song_of_Despair">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5407952-jibran?shelf=verse">verse</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5407952-jibran?shelf=nobel">nobel</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5407952-jibran?shelf=hispanic">hispanic</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1147252278" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17849222578814015170"><i>[Note on edit: This is not a review. These are peals of pleasure of a man drunk on Neruda wine, blurting out extempore, when he finished reading this poetry collection]</i><br/><br/>Pablo Neruda – the name evokes romance and revolution in my consciousness, a riot of metaphors impregnated with sui generis imagery, a dark and intense celebration of love and beauty, a flood of high emotions that assails my senses and then dulls them, such that in that state of mind I'm receptive to nothing in the world except Ne</span>
  <span id="freeText17849222578814015170" style="display:none"><i>[Note on edit: This is not a review. These are peals of pleasure of a man drunk on Neruda wine, blurting out extempore, when he finished reading this poetry collection]</i><br /><br />Pablo Neruda – the name evokes romance and revolution in my consciousness, a riot of metaphors impregnated with sui generis imagery, a dark and intense celebration of love and beauty, a flood of high emotions that assails my senses and then dulls them, such that in that state of mind I'm receptive to nothing in the world except Neruda's poetry. Everything else blacks out and I’m transported to a world I have never seen before – and it's beautiful, it is magnificent, it is dancing with the joy of love! <br /><br />I had never desired to learn Spanish, but after reading Neruda I wished I could find a way to experience him in the original, just as I wish I could improve my Persian to read Hafez and Rumi without the medium of translation. I really don't know how much of Neruda's Spanish is <i>lost in translation</i>, but whatever that has come down to us in English is more than sufficient to adore him.<br /><br />There is no one who so brilliantly marries nature's metaphors of earth, sea, wind, trees, moon, stars with the enchanting anatomy of the beloved. Every line testifies to Neruda's unique way of perceiving nature; he likens the beloved to nature, his beloved <i>becomes</i> nature. It is through meditations on the vast agricultural richness of his land that he finds the beloved, in the form of liberty, or in shape of an elusive woman, sometimes as an inextricable amalgamation of the two. They are inseparable.<br /><br />It is hard to make selections from this book; every poem is a work of wonder. Instead of copying many full-length poems, I am sampling some lines to show the luxuriant quality of imagery and the thunderous motion of his poems, the finesse of his thought, and the intensity of his style. Below are some of my favourite, quotable lines:<br /><br />The simple, fast and action-packed eroticism of the first lines of the opening poem, <b>Body of a woman</b>.<br /><br /><i>"Body of a woman, white hills, white thighs,<br />you look like a world, lying in surrender.<br />My rough peasant’s body digs in you<br />and makes the son leap from the depth of the earth."</i><br /><br />And see how, later on, from the 'white hills, white thighs', on which he gambols about with pleasure, she is transformed into a 'weapon' that offers him protection and provides him succor, through a process that remains a mystery to the poet and the reader:<br /><br /><i>"I was alone like a tunnel. The birds fled from me,<br />and night swamped me with its crushing invasion.<br />To survive myself I forged you like a weapon,<br />like an arrow in my bow, a stone in my sling."</i><br /><br /><br />In <b>'Almost Out of the Sky'</b> we have a 'cloudless girl', who shines like a clear sky, antithesis of greyness, an omniscient being whose presence is felt everywhere. But she is unknown and mysterious - she is a 'question of smoke', that appears and dissolves the next moment, without giving him a moment to regroup perceptions. She is as soft and silky as a 'corn tassel'. You can appreciate the finesse of this metaphor if you have pressed a corn tassel between your fingers! <br /><br />In this poem the beloved is cast into a formidable natural force that envelops and dominates the small and insignificant existence of the lover. He is in awe of her. This poem is asking to be quoted in full, without omission. So here it is:<br /><br /><i>"Almost out of the sky, half of the moon<br />anchors between two mountains.<br />Turning, wandering night, the digger of eyes.<br />Let’s see how many stars are smashed in the pool.<br /><br />It makes a cross of mourning between my eyes,<br />and runs away.<br />Forge of blue metals, nights of still combats,<br />my heart revolves like a crazy wheel.<br />Girl who have come from so far, been brought from so far,<br />sometimes your glance flashes out under the sky.<br />Rumbling, storm, cyclone of fury,<br />you cross above my heart without stopping.<br />Wind from the tombs carries off, wrecks, scatters your<br />sleepy root.<br /><br />The big trees on the other side of her, uprooted.<br />But you, cloudless girl, question of smoke, corn tassel.<br />You were what the wind was making with illuminated leaves.<br />Behind the nocturnal mountains, white lily of conflagration,<br />ah, I can say nothing! You were made of everything.<br /><br />Longing that sliced my breast into pieces,<br />it is time to take another road, on which she does not smile.<br /><br />Storm that buried the bells, muddy swirl of torments,<br />why touch her now, why make her sad.<br /><br />Oh to follow the road that leads away from everything,<br />without anguish, death, winter waiting along it<br />with their eyes open through the dew."</i><br /><br /><br />From <b>Every day you play</b>, Neruda finds the beloved in the most unlikely places. Holding a cluster of fruit is like holding beloved’s head:<br /><br /><i>"Every day you play with the light of the universe.<br />Subtle visitor, you arrive in the flower and the water.<br />You are more than this white head that I hold tightly<br />as a cluster of fruit, every day, between my hands.<br /><br />You are like nobody since I love you.<br />Let me spread you out among yellow garlands.<br />Who writes your name in letters of smoke among the stars<br />of the south?<br />Oh let me remember you as you were before you existed.<br /><br />Suddenly the wind howls and bangs at my shut window.<br />The sky is a net crammed with shadowy fish.<br />Here all the winds let go sooner or later, all of them.<br />The rain takes off her clothes."</i><br /><br />And further on:<br /><br /><i>"You are here. Oh, you do not run away.<br />You will answer me to the last cry.<br />Cling to me as though you were frightened.<br />Even so, at one time a strange shadow ran through your eyes.<br /><br />How you must have suffered getting accustomed to me,<br />my savage, solitary soul, my name that sends them all running.<br />So many times we have seen the morning star burn, kissing our eyes,<br />and over our heads the grey light unwind in turning fans."</i><br /><br />Neruda ends the poem with a striking image:<br /><br /><i>"I want<br />to do with you what spring does with the cherry trees."</i><br />--<br /><br />Originally posted 30/12/14</span>
  <a data-text-id="17849222578814015170" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText6170618056200395310" style="display:none">One of the most beautiful collection of love poems ever (and followed by one which will bring tears to your eyes), Neruda is clearly a master of language and feeling and I always derive comfort from every time I read this book. <br /><i>She loved me, sometimes I loved her.<br />How could I not have loved her large, still eyes?<br />I can write the saddest poem of all tonight.<br />To think I don’t have her. Love is so short, forgetting is so long. Because through nights like this one I held her in my arms my soul is not satisfied that it has lost her<br /><br />*Between the lips and the voice something goes dying.<br /> Something with the wings of a bird, <br />something of anguish and oblivion<br /><br />*Upstream, in the midst of the outer waves, your parallel body yields to my arms like a fish infinitely fastened to my soul, quick and slow, in the energy under the sky. <br /><br />"Leaning into the afternoons I fling my sad nets <br />to that sea that beats on your marine eyes."<br /><br />"The water walks barefoot in the wet streets.<br /> From that tree the leaves complain as though they were sick"<br /><br />*So that 'You Will Hear Me<br />But my words become stained with your love. <br />You occupy everything, you occupy everything.<br /><br /> I am making them into an endless necklace <br />for your white hands, smooth as grapes</span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/401945319?book_show_action=true">Aug 27, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David Schaafsma" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David Schaafsma" href="/user/show/269235-david-schaafsma">David Schaafsma</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/269235-david-schaafsma?shelf=poetry">poetry</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/269235-david-schaafsma?shelf=best-poetry-ever">best-poetry-ever</a>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer401945319" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer12551244814190089697">One Hundred Love Sonnets: XVII<br/>Pablo Neruda<br/><br/>Pablo Neruda was one of the great poets of the twentieth century, one of the great poets of all time—one of the great love poets, surrealist poets, political poets, poets of odes to common things.<br/><br/>I don’t love you as if you were a rose of salt, topaz, <br/>or arrow of carnations that propagate fire: <br/>I love you as one loves certain obscure things, <br/>secretly, between the shadow and the soul.<br/><br/>I love you as the plant that doesn’t bloom but carries <br/>the light of those</span>
  <span id="freeText12551244814190089697" style="display:none">One Hundred Love Sonnets: XVII<br />Pablo Neruda<br /><br />Pablo Neruda was one of the great poets of the twentieth century, one of the great poets of all time—one of the great love poets, surrealist poets, political poets, poets of odes to common things.<br /><br />I don’t love you as if you were a rose of salt, topaz, <br />or arrow of carnations that propagate fire: <br />I love you as one loves certain obscure things, <br />secretly, between the shadow and the soul.<br /><br />I love you as the plant that doesn’t bloom but carries <br />the light of those flowers, hidden, within itself, <br />and thanks to your love the tight aroma that arose <br />from the earth lives dimly in my body.<br /><br />I love you without knowing how, or when, or from where, <br />I love you directly without problems or pride:<br />I love you like this because I don’t know any other way to love,<br />except in this form in which I am not nor are you, <br />so close that your hand upon my chest is mine, <br />so close that your eyes close with my dreams.<br /><br />The film The Postman featured Neruda in Italy giving advice to a postman about how to win the love of a woman. Do you tell her she is beautiful? No. Do you tell her she is nice? No. Wonderful? No. So what is the answer?! Men, young and old, are waiting for the answer, Don Pablo!<br /><br />The answer: metaphor.<br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=8SMs8iC7khw">https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=8SMs8...</a><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="12551244814190089697" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
