    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1048227986" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1048227986" />
    <a title="B. P. Rinehart" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4635205-b-p-rinehart"><img alt="B. P. Rinehart" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1347740735p2/4635205.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1048227986?book_show_action=true">Sep 07, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="B. P. Rinehart" class="user" itemprop="url" name="B. P. Rinehart" href="/user/show/4635205-b-p-rinehart">B. P. Rinehart</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780142437704" href="/book/show/399095.Twenty_Love_Poems_and_a_Song_of_Despair">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4635205-b-p-rinehart?shelf=poetry-stuff">poetry-stuff</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4635205-b-p-rinehart?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1048227986" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9595206781992090070">This is a bilingual review: English first, then Spanish./Esta es una reseña bilingüe: inglés, luego español. (Muchas gracias, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/user/show/34207574-miquel-reina' rel='nofollow noopener'>Miquel</a>.)<br/><br/>"<i>Love is patient, love is kind. It does not envy, it does not boast, it is not proud. 5 It does not dishonor others, it is not self-seeking, it is not easily angered, it keeps no record of wrongs. 6 Love does not delight in evil but rejoices with the truth. 7 It always protects, always trusts, always hopes, always perseveres.</i>" - 1 Corinthians 13:4-7 by <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/6888271.Paul_of_Tarsus' title='Paul of Tarsus' rel='nofollow noopener'>Paul of Tar</a></span>
  <span id="freeText9595206781992090070" style="display:none">This is a bilingual review: English first, then Spanish./Esta es una reseña bilingüe: inglés, luego español. (Muchas gracias, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/show/34207574-miquel-reina" rel="nofollow noopener">Miquel</a>.)<br /><br />"<i>Love is patient, love is kind. It does not envy, it does not boast, it is not proud. 5 It does not dishonor others, it is not self-seeking, it is not easily angered, it keeps no record of wrongs. 6 Love does not delight in evil but rejoices with the truth. 7 It always protects, always trusts, always hopes, always perseveres.</i>" - 1 Corinthians 13:4-7 by <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/6888271.Paul_of_Tarsus" title="Paul of Tarsus" rel="nofollow noopener">Paul of Tarsus</a>.<br /><br />"<i>You know that language changes over a thousand years, and words that were then in use now seem strange to us; but they really did talk that way, and they spoke as eloquently about love as anyone did in any age or country.</i>" - Modern paraphrase from book 2 of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/820905.Troilus_and_Criseyde" title="Troilus and Criseyde by Geoffrey Chaucer" rel="nofollow noopener">Troilus and Criseyde</a> by <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/1838.Geoffrey_Chaucer" title="Geoffrey Chaucer" rel="nofollow noopener">Geoffrey Chaucer</a>. <br /><br />"<i>Love is a smoke raised with the fume of sighs;<br />Being purged, a fire sparkling in lovers' eyes;<br />Being vex'd a sea nourish'd with lovers' tears:<br />What is it else? a madness most discreet,<br />A choking gall and a preserving sweet.</i>" - <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/18135.Romeo_and_Juliet" title="Romeo and Juliet by William Shakespeare" rel="nofollow noopener">Romeo and Juliet</a> Act 1, scene 1 by <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/947.William_Shakespeare" title="William Shakespeare" rel="nofollow noopener">William Shakespeare</a>.<br /><br />"<i>Just as time knew to move on since the beginning<br />And the seasons know exactly when to change<br />Just as kindness knows no shame<br />Know through all your joy and pain<br />That I'll be loving you always</i>." - From the song "As" by <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/397584.Stevie_Wonder" title="Stevie Wonder" rel="nofollow noopener">Stevie Wonder</a>.<br /><br /><br />As the above quotes show, humans have been trying to define "love" forever. It is a concept that we contemplate on in some way, shape, or form, constantly with varying levels of success or basic understanding. I was content long ago to let St. Paul have the definitive word on this topic...until I met Señor Pablo Neruda. I did not think it was possible to really be able to define love more than once. Pablo Neruda successfully does it...twenty times! The edition of this book I read is a dual-translation, but my quotes from it will be in english and from the poem that made me pick it up: "Juegas Todos Los Días":<br /><blockquote>
  <i>"Every day you play with the light of the universe.<br />Subtle visitor, you arrive in the flower and the water,<br />You are more than this white head that I hold tightly<br />as a bunch of flowers, every day, between my hands.<br /><br />You are like nobody since I love you.<br />Let me spread you out among yellow garlands.<br />Who writes your name in letters of smoke among the stars of the south?<br />Oh let me remember you as you were before you existed.<br /><br />Suddenly the wind howls and bangs at my shut window.<br />The sky is a net crammed with shadowy fish.<br />Here all the winds let go sooner or later, all of them.<br />The rain takes off her clothes."</i>
</blockquote>This poem hit me like a brick and I wanted to hear more and learn about the man who wrote it. Pablo Neruda was an almost unknown poet from Chile who shot to instant stardom when he published this volume of poetry. He would be the second internationally known South American writer after neighboring Argentinian <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/500.Jorge_Luis_Borges" title="Jorge Luis Borges" rel="nofollow noopener">Jorge Luis Borges</a>. He was also a socialist who developed a close bond with the world's first democratically-elected Marxist president <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/10102.Salvador_Allende" title="Salvador Allende" rel="nofollow noopener">Salvador Allende</a>. Neruda would die days after Allende's death and the aftermath of the coup against Allende by General Agusto Pinochet.<br /><blockquote>
  <i>"The birds go by, fleeing.<br />The wind. The wind.<br />I alone can contend against the power of men.<br />The storm whirls dark leaves<br />and turns loose all the boats that were moored last night to the sky.<br /><br />You are here. Oh, you do not run away.<br />You will answer me to the last cry.<br />Curl round me as though you were frightened.<br />Even so, a strange shadow once ran through your eyes.<br /><br />Now, now too, little one, you bring me honeysuckle,<br />and even your breasts smell of it.<br />While the sad wind goes slaughtering butterflies<br />I love you, and my happiness bites the plum of your mouth."</i>
</blockquote>These poems are so unashamed and forthright that it is almost shocking to think this collection was published in 1924. Neruda is not at all embarrassed to talk about love and he feels the utmost happiness and joy in each of his poems. His use of imagery would give <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/18540.T_S__Eliot" title="T.S. Eliot" rel="nofollow noopener">T.S. Eliot</a> a run for his money and he does not give you a weak poem in the bunch. Even the "Song of Despair" at the end is still at the same high passionate intensity as the preceding 20 poems. Whether you are in love or use to be in love (speaking for myself), you will appreciate this book's honest devotion and declaration to this ancient and yet new concept.<br /><br />"<i>How you must have suffered getting accustomed to me,<br />my savage, solitary soul, my name that sends them all running.<br />So many times we have seen the morning star burn, kissing our eyes,<br />and over our heads the grey light unwinds in turning fans.<br /><br />My words rained over you, stroking you.<br />A long time I have loved the sunned mother-of-pearl of your body.<br />Until I even believe that you own the universe.<br />I will bring you happy flowers from the mountains, bluebells, dark hazels, and rustic baskets of kisses.<br />I want to do with you what spring does with the cherry trees.</i>" -Poema XIV <br /><br /><br />Desde siempre el hombre ha intentado definir el "amor". Es un concepto que contemplamos constantemente de muchas maneras, formas o figuras, con diferentes grados de éxito o de comprensión. Hace tiempo estaba contento con la manera en qué San Pablo definía este este tema... hasta que conocí el señor Pablo Neruda. No creía que fuera posible o capaces de definir el amor más de una vez. En cambio Pablo Neruda lo hace con éxito veinte veces! La edición de este libro que leí es una doble traducción, pero mis citas del poema "Juegas Todos Los Días" que recojo a continuación son en español:<br /><i>
  <blockquote>"Juegas todos los días con la luz del universo. Sutil visitadora, llegas en la flor y en el agua. Eres más que esta blanca cabecita que aprieto como un racimo entre mis manos cada día.<br /><br />A nadie te pareces desde que yo te amo. Déjame tenderte entre guirnaldas amarillas. Quién escribe tu nombre con letras de humo entre las estrellas del sur? Ah déjame recordarte cómo eras entonces, cuando aún no existías.<br /><br />De pronto el viento aúlla y golpea mi ventana cerrada. El cielo es una red cuajada de peces sombríos. Aquí vienen a dar todos los vientos, todos. Se desviste la lluvia."</blockquote>
</i><br />Este poema me golpeó como un ladrillo y quería saber más y aprender sobre el hombre que lo escribió. Pablo Neruda fue un poeta casi desconocido de Chile que saltó a la fama instantánea cuando publicó este volumen de poesía. Él sería el segundo escritor sudamericano más conocido internacionalmente después del vecino argentino Jorge Luis Borges. También fue un socialista que desarrolló un estrecho vínculo con el primer presidente marxista elegido democráticamente en el mundo, Salvador Allende. Neruda moriría días después de la muerte de Allende y las consecuencias del golpe de Estado contra Allende por el general Augusto Pinochet. <br /><i>
  <blockquote>"Pasan huyendo los pájaros. El viento. El viento. Yo sólo puedo luchar contra la fuerza de los hombres. El temporal arremolina hojas oscuras y suelta todas las barcas que anoche amarraron al cielo.<br /><br />Tú estás aquí. Ah tú no huyes. Tú me responderás hasta el último grito. Ovíllate a mi lado como si tuvieras miedo. Sin embargo alguna vez corrió una sombra extraña por tus ojos.<br /><br />Ahora, ahora también, pequeña, me traes madreselvas, y tienes hasta los senos perfumados. Mientras el viento triste galopa matando mariposas yo te amo, y mi alegría muerde tu boca de ciruela."</blockquote>
</i><br />Estos poemas son tan desvergonzados y directos que es casi chocante pensar que se publicaron en 1924. Pero Neruda no siente en absoluto vergüenza al hablar sobre el amor y una gran felicidad y alegría se transmite en cada uno de sus poemas. Su uso de imágenes daría T.S. Eliot un plazo para su dinero y él no le da un poema débil en el pelotón. Incluso la "canción desesperada" al final se encuentra todavía en el mismo nivel de intensidad apasionada como los 20 poemas anteriores. Si estás enamorado o lo has estado (éste es mi caso), podrás apreciar este libro de sincera y devota declaración a este concepto tan antiguo pero a la vez tan nuevo.<br /><br />"<i>Cuanto te habrá dolido acostumbrarte a mí, a mi alma sola y salvaje, a mi nombre que todos ahuyentan. Hemos visto arder tantas veces el lucero besándonos los ojos y sobre nuestras cabezas destorcerse los crepúsculos en abanicos girantes.<br /><br />Mis palabras llovieron sobre ti acariciándote. Amé desde hace tiempo tu cuerpo de nácar soleado. Hasta te creo dueña del universo. Te traeré de las montañas flores alegres, copihues, avellanas oscuras, y cestas silvestres de besos. <br /><br />Quiero hacer contigo lo que la primavera hace con los cerezos.</i>" - Poema XIV</span>
  <a data-text-id="9595206781992090070" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Apoorva" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Apoorva" href="/user/show/25605420-apoorva">Apoorva</a>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2553236253" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer11606354901848865704">Beautiful and sensual with a touch of lingering sadness.<br/>One of my favs:<br/><br/>Tonight I can write the saddest lines.<br/><br/>Write, for example,'The night is shattered<br/>and the blue stars shiver in the distance.'<br/><br/>The night wind revolves in the sky and sings.<br/><br/>Tonight I can write the saddest lines.<br/>I loved her, and sometimes she loved me too.<br/><br/>Through nights like this one I held her in my arms<br/>I kissed her again and again under the endless sky.<br/><br/>She loved me sometimes, and I loved her too.<br/>How could one not have loved her g</span>
  <span id="freeText11606354901848865704" style="display:none">Beautiful and sensual with a touch of lingering sadness.<br />One of my favs:<br /><br />Tonight I can write the saddest lines.<br /><br />Write, for example,'The night is shattered<br />and the blue stars shiver in the distance.'<br /><br />The night wind revolves in the sky and sings.<br /><br />Tonight I can write the saddest lines.<br />I loved her, and sometimes she loved me too.<br /><br />Through nights like this one I held her in my arms<br />I kissed her again and again under the endless sky.<br /><br />She loved me sometimes, and I loved her too.<br />How could one not have loved her great still eyes.<br /><br />Tonight I can write the saddest lines.<br />To think that I do not have her. To feel that I have lost her.<br /><br />To hear the immense night, still more immense without her.<br />And the verse falls to the soul like dew to the pasture.<br /><br />What does it matter that my love could not keep her.<br />The night is shattered and she is not with me.<br /><br />This is all. In the distance someone is singing. In the distance.<br />My soul is not satisfied that it has lost her.<br /><br />My sight searches for her as though to go to her.<br />My heart looks for her, and she is not with me.<br /><br />The same night whitening the same trees.<br />We, of that time, are no longer the same.<br /><br />I no longer love her, that's certain, but how I loved her.<br />My voice tried to find the wind to touch her hearing.<br /><br />Another's. She will be another's. Like my kisses before.<br />Her voice. Her bright body. Her infinite eyes.<br /><br />I no longer love her, that's certain, but maybe I love her. <br />Love is so short, forgetting is so long.<br /><br />Because through nights like this one I held her in my arms<br />my soul is not satisfied that it has lost her.<br /><br />Though this be the last pain that she makes me suffer<br />and these the last verses that I write for her.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11606354901848865704" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780142437704" href="/book/show/399095.Twenty_Love_Poems_and_a_Song_of_Despair">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">those who appreciate being steeped in imagery... &amp; passion.</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/332925-whichwaydidshego?shelf=translations">translations</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/332925-whichwaydidshego?shelf=poetry">poetry</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer8101450" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5562724587292539160">I took my time reading this, choosing to savor the succulent, vivid, tactile words. I must say, these poems are luscious! I <i>feel</i> their imagery as much as visualize it. Phrases such as "In the moist night my garment of kisses trembles..." A garment of kisses. How delightful! (I want one!)<br/><br/>I also love how he is constantly mixing ideas of fire and water together, as if with love somehow they feed off each other where they should cancel each other out. "Bonfire of awe in which my thirst was burning."</span>
  <span id="freeText5562724587292539160" style="display:none">I took my time reading this, choosing to savor the succulent, vivid, tactile words. I must say, these poems are luscious! I <i>feel</i> their imagery as much as visualize it. Phrases such as "In the moist night my garment of kisses trembles..." A garment of kisses. How delightful! (I want one!)<br /><br />I also love how he is constantly mixing ideas of fire and water together, as if with love somehow they feed off each other where they should cancel each other out. "Bonfire of awe in which my thirst was burning." "...I go mounded on my one wave,/lunar, solar, burning and cold all at once." It is a delicate balance that is dynamic as the flames of passion grow and the cresting waves of excitement rise and crash in. <br /><br />As a side note, I love that the poem is shown in it's original language first and on the opposing page. It's intriguingly seductive to read the verse in it's original form and hear it's fluidity and elegance. Then, too, having the Picasso illustrations intermingled with Neruda's vibrant voice adds volume to the juxtaposition of his colliding passions.<br /><br />This is a lovely little tome that will envelop you and draw you back into it's pages with words like, "You undermine the horizon with your absence," calling out to the core of you.</span>

  <span id="freeText17708461842243008175" style="display:none">I'm not a good person at judging poetry, but thought I'd give this one a go after hearing it recommended on a Bookish podcast.<br /><br />I thought it was cool how it had the original Spanish version next to the translated one. I wish I knew Spanish because I bet this was even better in its intended form, however the translator did an excellent job with it. I also enjoyed the introduction because I got to learn some basics about Pablo Neruda - his life and work. <br /><br />It was very short - literally 20 love poems and an extra one titled "A Song of Despair". I'm horrible at deciphering poetry, the imagery, meaning, symbolism, etc... But what I gathered from this was that it was beautifully crafted and very sensual. Particularly since it was published in 1924, I was kind of blown away with some of it. It was packed full of all the human senses and I got a overwhelming sense of loneliness throughout much of the poems. I would definitely try some more by this poet.</span>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2437602682" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2437602682" />
    <a title="Roy Lotz" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/14046996-roy-lotz"><img alt="Roy Lotz" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1443324405p2/14046996.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2437602682?book_show_action=true">Jun 27, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Roy Lotz" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Roy Lotz" href="/user/show/14046996-roy-lotz">Roy Lotz</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9789706660718" href="/book/show/833096.20_poemas_de_amor_y_una_canci_n_desesperada">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14046996-roy-lotz?shelf=no-hablo-ingl%C3%A9s">no-hablo-inglés</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14046996-roy-lotz?shelf=best-words-best-order">best-words-best-order</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2437602682" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2772389752947729194"><blockquote>
  <i>Oh, the power to celebrate you with all the words of happiness<br/>To sing, to burn, to flee, like a church bell in the hands of a madman.</i>
</blockquote><br/>Pablo Neruda is the most famous Spanish-speaking poet of the 20th century, perhaps in all of literature. He published this, his most popular book, when he was 19 years old—a fact which will fill you with hope or despair, depending on your age. Yet it is youth (being the period in which love is felt most and understood least) that is the best time to write love </span>
  <span id="freeText2772389752947729194" style="display:none"><blockquote>
  <i>Oh, the power to celebrate you with all the words of happiness<br />To sing, to burn, to flee, like a church bell in the hands of a madman.</i>
</blockquote><br />Pablo Neruda is the most famous Spanish-speaking poet of the 20th century, perhaps in all of literature. He published this, his most popular book, when he was 19 years old—a fact which will fill you with hope or despair, depending on your age. Yet it is youth (being the period in which love is felt most and understood least) that is the best time to write love poetry, as Neruda’s case proves.<br /><br />I found this book to be more difficult than I expected. In structure the poems range from metrical rhyming couplets to something approaching free-verse. For vocabulary, Neruda mainly sticks to the romantic poetic idiom developed in the previous century (every language has its own specialized stock of poetic words, it seems) and so the poems required some deciphering for me to understand them. But neither of these presented a real difficulty. Rather, what challenged me was that Neruda is a sensual poet. To a degree all poets are sensual, of course; but some are more so than others. As a contrast I would offer Antonio Machado, whose work I read just before this book, and whose poetry usually contains a conceptual core, a kernel of an idea, wrapped up within the images of the poem, which I could at least partially uncover. Neruda’s poems, by contrast, are driven by a touch rather than a thought.<br /><br />Now, I do not want to stress this dichotomy too much. It is one of the mysteries of the human mind that nothing can be thought without eliciting a feeling, and nothing can be felt without eliciting a thought; and that moods and beliefs, so apparently separate, are really deeply intertwined. Without this deep bond between concept and affect, poetry would hardly be possible, seeing as it plays on the hidden cords that stretch between our sensory, cognitive, and emotional worlds, finding strange harmonies in disparate sources, revealing hitherto unimagined connections in our inner architecture. And since love—and its dark twin, loss—resides at the center of this dusty network, Neruda has much to work with.<br /><blockquote>
  <i>I was alone like a tunnel. The birds fled from me<br />and the night entered me with its powerful invasion.<br />To survive, I forged you like a weapon,<br />like an arrow in my bow, like a stone in my sling.<br /><br />But now falls the hour of vengeance, and I love you.<br />Body of skin, of moss, of avid and firm milk.</i>
</blockquote><br />Translating his lines fails to do justice to Neruda, as it fails to do justice to any poet. This is not to say that poetry is absolutely untranslatable; too many poets have found success in foreign tongues for that to be believed. Nevertheless, something is obviously lost. To pick just one example, in one poem Neruda says he wants to make an infinite chain <i>“para tus manos blancas, suaves como las uvas,”</i> which translates to “for your white hands, soft like grapes.” To my ears, this line hardly works in English. For one, the parallel contour of <i>“suave”</i> and <i>“uva”</i> is lost; and more important, the word <i>“uva”</i> is a gentle-sounding word, while “grape” is harsh and grating. Situations like this happen often in translation, presenting the translator with a choice between literal or impressionistic fidelity; and both choices have their downsides.<br /><br />As you might expect of youthful love poetry, this book is full of ardor, of lust, of desperation, and of the deepest tenderness. The sky is pulled apart, the sea is drained, the stars are rearranged, the forests are uprooted, and the world itself is bent into new shapes in Neruda’s attempt to express his heart. Not a note rings false; there is much sentiment here but little sentimentality. Neruda (mostly) avoids self-pity—that curse of adolescence—and he uses personal metaphors to translate private feelings into universal experiences. Poetry could hardly strive for more. <br /><blockquote>
  <i>I like it when you’re still because it’s like you’re absent.<br />Distant and painful as if you were dead.<br />One word, then, one smile is enough.<br />And I am happy, happy because it is not true.</i>
</blockquote></span>
  <a data-text-id="2772389752947729194" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

<span id="freeTextContainer12768886428894146748">Neruda does not play with the intangible. He does not waste words with the abstract. One simply needs to read and take in the pure and stark versification of the sensualities of life, both in love and lust. <br/><br/>Neruda’s distinct style in poetry is easily distinguishable. <br/><br/><b>First,</b> his work is intuitive of the austere beauty of nature and his Chilean roots. The verses are reflective of the uncompromising beauty of the environment that he has witnessed in his formative years. The poems allude to the <i> vas</i></span>
  <span id="freeText12768886428894146748" style="display:none">Neruda does not play with the intangible. He does not waste words with the abstract. One simply needs to read and take in the pure and stark versification of the sensualities of life, both in love and lust. <br><br>Neruda’s distinct style in poetry is easily distinguishable. <br><br><b>First,</b> his work is intuitive of the austere beauty of nature and his Chilean roots. The verses are reflective of the uncompromising beauty of the environment that he has witnessed in his formative years. The poems allude to the <i> vastness of the pines, the heart of summer, sweet blue hyacinths, still ponds, barren lands, and white bees. </i><br><br><b>
  <blockquote>“I will bring you happy flowers from the mountains, <br>bluebells,<br>dark hazels, and rustic basket of kisses.”<br>(74, Poem XIV) </blockquote>
</b><br><br><b>Second,</b> Neruda also leads us to enjoy the sweetness existing in realm of the senses. He fearlessly incorporates love and lust in his verses. <br><br><blockquote>
  <b>“My somber heart searches for you, nevertheless,<br>And I love your joyful body, your slender and flowing voice.”<br> (75, Poem XIX)<br><br>“Tonight I can write the saddest lines.<br>I loved her, and sometimes she loved me too.” <br>(77, Poem XX)</b>
</blockquote><br><br>But to read and consume these two aspects of his poetry in a compartmentalized manner would be an affront to why Gabriel Garcia Marquez called Neruda <b>
  <i>“the greatest poet of the 20th century in any language."</i>
</b>* Neruda combines the sensual experience of the individual with the beauty of the natural and the reader is treated to a union unlike any other.<br><br><blockquote>
  <b>“Body of a woman, white hills, white thighs<br>You look like a world, lying in surrender.<br>My rough peasant’s body digs in you<br>and makes the son leap from the depth of the earth.” <br>(3 Poem I)<br><br>“I go so far a to think that you own the universe.<br>I will bring you happy flowers from the mountains, <br>bluebells,<br>dark hazels, and rustic basket of kisses.<br>I want<br>to do with you what spring does with the cherry trees.”<br>(74, Poem XIV)</b>
</blockquote><br><br><br>notes:<br>* <a href="http://books.google.com/books?id=oIpdAAAAMAAJ" rel="nofollow noopener">The fragrance of guava: Conversations with Gabriel García Márquez. </a><br><br>I did not give a short introduction on Neruda reserving most of my comments later on for a review on his memoirs.<br><br>My copy is bilingual, a Spanish-English translation by W.S. Wermin, which definitely polished my rusting Spanish speaking skills. <br><br>The same copy is infused with Pablo Picasso’s works like this, <br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1402231201i/9910054.jpg" width="400" height="400" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>You get the idea that it seeks to perhaps contribute to the general them of the book, but I have no sound knowledge if this was sanctioned or approved by Neruda in its first translated printing in 1969, five years before he died, or whether the same pictures accompanied the first print in Chile in 1924, or if it appeared only in this copy published by Penguin Books. <br><br><br><br><br><br>This book forms part of my remarkably extensive reading list on <a href="http://imbookedindefinitely.blogspot.com/2014/06/the-nobel-project.html" rel="nofollow noopener">Nobel Prize for Literature Laureates</a><br><br>This review, along with my other reviews, has been cross-posted at <a href="http://imbookedindefinitely.blogspot.com/" rel="nofollow noopener">imbookedindefinitely</a></span>

<div id="review_2378590389" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2378590389" />
    <a title="Antonio" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/29384668-antonio"><img alt="Antonio" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1548566399p2/29384668.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2378590389?book_show_action=true">May 01, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Antonio" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Antonio" href="/user/show/29384668-antonio">Antonio</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/29384668-antonio?shelf=read-in-2018">read-in-2018</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/29384668-antonio?shelf=nobel-winners">nobel-winners</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/29384668-antonio?shelf=poetry">poetry</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2378590389" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10855382514419749918"><i>“Tonight I can write the saddest lines<br/>I loved her, and sometimes she loved me too.” </i><br/><br/>I'm still drunk on Neruda's poems and to be honest, I'm not sure I'll sober up again anytime soon. The author's words seem to draw you into a kind of trance in which you start to say the poems out loud, creating a mixture of the poet's feelings and yours. You then keep the trance by listening to your own words, Neruda's words spoken through your tongue; the sound that could hypnotize you easily till dawn.<br/><br/><i> “I am n</i></span>
  <span id="freeText10855382514419749918" style="display:none"><i>“Tonight I can write the saddest lines<br />I loved her, and sometimes she loved me too.” </i><br /><br />I'm still drunk on Neruda's poems and to be honest, I'm not sure I'll sober up again anytime soon. The author's words seem to draw you into a kind of trance in which you start to say the poems out loud, creating a mixture of the poet's feelings and yours. You then keep the trance by listening to your own words, Neruda's words spoken through your tongue; the sound that could hypnotize you easily till dawn.<br /><br /><i> “I am no longer in love with her, that's certain, but maybe I love her. Love is so short, forgetting is so long.” </i><br /><br />So is this masterpiece. A short book whose forgetting is an almost impossible task. Actually, why would anyone want to do it? In the beginning, there is a delightful and impressive introduction written by Cristina García. In her text, she tells us a little about the poet's life, as well as the impact he had on her life. I can clearly understand why he has changed her existence and although I cannot say the same (at least for now), Neruda did make my day and probably my week better. <br /><br /><i> “It was at that age that poetry came in search of me.” </i><br /><br />As Neruda several times recited his poems to thousands of people, I also declaim them out loud today at home. Playing fair, this is a strong habit of mine: most of my acquaintances - at least the ones I care about - have already received an audio of a poem, a chronic or an excerpt of a book. With Neruda's work, however, it came to me even more naturally as if his work was not made to remain still, written in an old sheet of paper. It claimed to be alive once again.<br /><br /><i> “There were thirst and hunger, and you were the fruit.<br />There were grief and the ruins, and you were the miracle.” </i><br /><br />The miracle of love. The miracle of knowing we are still capable of love even after harsh and painful times. The miracle of realizing there is still hope. An outstanding must-read for anyone who wants to imbibe on poetry.<br /><br /><i> “Every day you play with the light of the universe.” </i></span>
  <a data-text-id="10855382514419749918" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <div class="left bodycol">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2706514382" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer14772146010323433365">Poems that I love<br/>The Cataract of Lodore<br/>BY ROBERT SOUTHEY <br/><br/> From its sources which well<br/><br/> In the tarn on the fell;<br/><br/> From its fountains<br/><br/> In the mountains,<br/><br/> Its rills and its gills;<br/><br/> Through moss and through brake,<br/><br/> It runs and it creeps<br/><br/> For a while, till it sleeps<br/><br/> In its own little lake.<br/><br/> And thence at departing,<br/><br/> Awakening and starting,<br/><br/> It runs through the reeds,<br/><br/> And away it proceeds,<br/><br/> Through meadow and glade,<br/><br/> In sun and in shade,<br/><br/> And through the wood-shelter,<br/><br/> Among crags in its flurry,<br/><br/> Helter-skelter,<br/><br/> Hur</span>
  <span id="freeText14772146010323433365" style="display:none">Poems that I love<br />The Cataract of Lodore<br />BY ROBERT SOUTHEY <br /><br /> From its sources which well<br /><br /> In the tarn on the fell;<br /><br /> From its fountains<br /><br /> In the mountains,<br /><br /> Its rills and its gills;<br /><br /> Through moss and through brake,<br /><br /> It runs and it creeps<br /><br /> For a while, till it sleeps<br /><br /> In its own little lake.<br /><br /> And thence at departing,<br /><br /> Awakening and starting,<br /><br /> It runs through the reeds,<br /><br /> And away it proceeds,<br /><br /> Through meadow and glade,<br /><br /> In sun and in shade,<br /><br /> And through the wood-shelter,<br /><br /> Among crags in its flurry,<br /><br /> Helter-skelter,<br /><br /> Hurry-skurry.<br /><br /> Here it comes sparkling,<br /><br /> And there it lies darkling;<br /><br /> Now smoking and frothing<br /><br /> Its tumult and wrath in,<br /><br /> Till, in this rapid race<br /><br /> On which it is bent,<br /><br /> It reaches the place<br /><br /> Of its steep descent.<br /><br /> <br /><br /> The cataract strong<br /><br /> Then plunges along,<br /><br /> Striking and raging<br /><br /> <br /><br /> As if a war raging<br /><br /> Its caverns and rocks among;<br /><br /> Rising and leaping,<br /><br /> Sinking and creeping,<br /><br /> Swelling and sweeping,<br /><br /> Showering and springing,<br /><br /> Flying and flinging,<br /><br /> Writhing and ringing,<br /><br /> Eddying and whisking,<br /><br /> Spouting and frisking,<br /><br /> Turning and twisting,<br /><br /> Around and around<br /><br /> With endless rebound:<br /><br /> Smiting and fighting,<br /><br /> A sight to delight in;<br /><br /> Confounding, astounding,<br /><br /> Dizzying and deafening the ear with its sound.<br /><br /> <br />Collecting, projecting,<br /><br /> Receding and speeding,<br /><br /> And shocking and rocking,<br /><br /> And darting and parting,<br /><br /> And threading and spreading,<br /><br /> And whizzing and hissing,<br /><br /> And dripping and skipping,<br /><br /> And hitting and splitting,<br /><br /> And shining and twining,<br /><br /> And rattling and battling,<br /><br /> And shaking and quaking,<br /><br /> And pouring and roaring,<br /><br /> And waving and raving,<br /><br /> And tossing and crossing,<br /><br /> And flowing and going,<br /><br /> And running and stunning,<br /><br /> And foaming and roaming,<br /><br /> And dinning and spinning,<br /><br /> And dropping and hopping,<br /><br /> And working and jerking,<br /><br /> And guggling and struggling,<br /><br /> And heaving and cleaving,<br /><br /> And moaning and groaning;<br /><br /> And glittering and frittering,<br /><br /> And gathering and feathering,<br /><br /> And whitening and brightening,<br /><br /> And quivering and shivering,<br /><br /> And hurrying and skurrying,<br /><br /> And thundering and floundering;<br /><br />  Dividing and gliding and sliding,<br /><br /> And falling and brawling and sprawling,<br /><br /> And driving and riving and striving,<br /><br /> And sprinkling and twinkling and wrinkling,<br /><br /> And sounding and bounding and rounding,<br /><br /> And bubbling and troubling and doubling,<br /><br /> And grumbling and rumbling and tumbling,<br /><br /> And clattering and battering and shattering;<br /><br /> Retreating and beating and meeting and sheeting,<br /><br /> Delaying and straying and playing and spraying,<br /><br /> Advancing and prancing and glancing and dancing,<br /><br /> Recoiling, turmoiling and toiling and boiling,<br /><br /><br /> And gleaming and streaming and steaming and beaming,<br /><br /> And rushing and flushing and brushing and gushing,<br /><br /> And flapping and rapping and clapping and slapping,<br /><br /> And curling and whirling and purling and twirling,<br /><br /> And thumping and plumping and bumping and jumping,<br /><br /> And dashing and flashing and splashing and clashing;<br /><br /> And so never ending, but always descending,<br /><br /> Sounds and motions for ever and ever are blending<br /><br /> All at once and all o'er, with a mighty uproar, -<br /><br /> And this way the water comes down at Lodore.<br /><br /><br /><br />Continuities<br /><br />Walt Whitman, 1819 - 1892<br /><br /> Nothing is ever really lost, or can be lost, No birth, identity, form—no object of the world. Nor life, nor force, nor any visible thing; Appearance must not foil, nor shifted sphere confuse thy brain. Ample are time and space—ample the fields of Nature. The body, sluggish, aged, cold—the embers left from earlier fires, The light in the eye grown dim, shall duly flame again; The sun now low in the west rises for mornings and for noons continual; To frozen clods ever the spring's invisible law returns, With grass and flowers and summer fruits and corn.<br /><br /><br />Each Note by Rumi<br /><br />Advice doesn’t help lovers!<br />They’re not the kind of mountain stream<br />you can build a dam across.<br /><br />An intellectual doesn’t know<br />what the drunk is feeling!<br /><br />Don’t try to figure<br />what those lost inside love<br />will do next!<br /><br />Someone in charge would give up all his power,<br />if he caught one whiff of the wine-musk<br />from the room where the lovers<br />are doing who-knows-what!<br /><br />One of them tries to dig a hole through a mountain.<br />One flees from academic honors.<br />One laughs at famous mustaches!<br /><br />Life freezes if it doesn’t get a taste<br />of this almond cake.<br />The stars come up spinning<br />every night, bewildered in love.<br />They’d grow tired<br />with that revolving, if they weren’t.<br />They’d say,<br />“How long do we have to do this!”<br /><br />God picks up the reed-flute world and blows.<br />Each note is a need coming through one of us,<br />a passion, a longing-pain.<br />Remember the lips<br />where the wind-breath originated,<br />and let your note be clear.<br />Don’t try to end it.<br />Be your note.<br />I’ll show you how it’s enough.<br /><br />Go up on the roof at night<br />in this city of the soul.<br /><br />Let everyone climb on their roofs<br />and sing their notes!<br />Sing loud!<br /><br /><br />Ok the reason for showing these to start my 'review' of Pablo Nerudas 20 poems....(which I chose to read for the Valentine's day romantic challenge that the group Reading for pleasure are hosting) is to show that I do indeed like poetry in it's many guises. I have numerous volumes on my bookcase including obviously Rumi, Whitman and Southey but also Wordsworth, Yeats, Emily Bronte and Sylvia Plath oh and Margaret Atwood. So I'm not a novice, although I do not profess to be proficient. <br />I realise poetry is subjective and that my idea of beautiful, lyrical, emotive, rousing, passionate poetry is going to be different from other peoples but .. this book had me recoiling at the metaphors used for female body parts and calling his lover a toy doll, this is not love, it's the thoughts of a teenage boy whose bed sheets need washing in the morning!!! <br />I know this is not the general consensus when it comes to this book but for me poems, the ones I love, get into your soul and these didn't work for me at all. <br />Very disappointed. 1*</span>
  <a data-text-id="14772146010323433365" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer17742018312253301038">Quick Update, 7-8-17:<br/>I didn't intend to review this again. In honesty, I'm trying to hit 150 for complete reads this year, which makes about four per week. I own a copy of this now and I knew how quickly I could read it. I'm shocked, stunned, mesmerized.   <span id="freeText17742018312253301038" style="display:none">Quick Update, 7-8-17:<br />I didn't intend to review this again. In honesty, I'm trying to hit 150 for complete reads this year, which makes about four per week. I own a copy of this now and I knew how quickly I could read it. I'm shocked, stunned, mesmerized. I read some of his other writings and they didn't impact me the same, so I put Neruda at 20 on my list. Now I've moved him up to number 2, just under Kafka. The collection reads like a love story: a man lives a rough life, and a woman helps learn to live and love, and the painful resolution to the plot comes, but the story comes from his own life. He almost had me in tears because most of us can relate to being in love, going through difficulties and facing the inevitable end of all of us. <br /><br />-------------------------------------<br /><br />I've finally found an author who makes me feel the way Kafka makes me feel, in a different, but equally powerful way.<br /><br />Rather than mull over each word I get into this canoe and Neruda paddles and sweeps me along this river path of love's delight and heartbreak, and intertwined in his memories I see flashes of personal memory.<br /><br />What a powerful, deep, intelligent and beautiful soul!<br /><br />Absolutely a favorite! You can't reverse engineer this! :-)</span>

<div id="review_1589869505" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1589869505" />
    <a title="Fareya" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/22583912-fareya"><img alt="Fareya" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1485406498p2/22583912.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1589869505?book_show_action=true">Apr 12, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Fareya" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Fareya" href="/user/show/22583912-fareya">Fareya</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22583912-fareya?shelf=poetry">poetry</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1589869505" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2144410986026531694"><i>"Always, always you recede through the evenings<br/>towards where the twilight goes erasing statues." </i><br/><br/>An enduring collection of exquisite verses. Even though translated from Spanish, these words sound eloquent and lyrical.<br/><br/>Simple, sensual, beautiful words filled with tenderness and a vivid imagination.<br/><br/><i>"I will bring you happy flowers from the mountains, <br/>bluebells,<br/>dark hazels, and rustic basket of kisses.<br/>I want<br/>to do with you what spring does with the cherry trees."</i><br/><br/>Lush, rich, intense words filled with r</span>
  <span id="freeText2144410986026531694" style="display:none"><i>"Always, always you recede through the evenings<br />towards where the twilight goes erasing statues." </i><br /><br />An enduring collection of exquisite verses. Even though translated from Spanish, these words sound eloquent and lyrical.<br /><br />Simple, sensual, beautiful words filled with tenderness and a vivid imagination.<br /><br /><i>"I will bring you happy flowers from the mountains, <br />bluebells,<br />dark hazels, and rustic basket of kisses.<br />I want<br />to do with you what spring does with the cherry trees."</i><br /><br />Lush, rich, intense words filled with reverence and longing.<br /><br /><i>"As all things are filled with my soul<br />You emerge from the things<br />Filled with my soul<br />You are like my soul<br />A butterfly of dream<br />And you are like the word: Melancholy<br /><br />I like for you to be still<br />And you seem far away<br />It sounds as though you are lamenting<br />A butterfly cooing like a dove<br />And you hear me from far away<br />And my voice does not reach you<br />Let me come to be still in your silence"</i><br /><br />Passionate, evocative, haunting words filled with a burning desperation.<br /><br /><i>"I no longer love her, that's certain, but maybe I love her. <br />Love is so short, forgetting is so long. <br />Because through nights like this one I held her in my arms <br />my soul is not satisfied that it has lost her. <br />Though this is the last pain that she makes me suffer <br />and these the last verses that I write for her."</i><br /><br />The song of despair at the end is an assortment of heart breaking, soul shattering words that speak of a sizzling fiery torment.<br /><br /><i>"The memory of you emerges from the night around me.<br />The river mingles its stubborn lament with the sea.<br />Deserted like the wharves at dawn.<br />It is the hour of departure, oh deserted one!<br />Cold flower heads are raining over my heart.<br />Oh pit of debris, fierce cave of the shipwrecked.<br />In you the wars and the flights accumulated.<br />From you the wings of the song birds rose.<br />You swallowed everything, like distance.<br />Like the sea, like time. In you everything sank!"</i><br /><br />It is no wonder Pablo Neruda was awarded the Nobel Prize for Literature. <br /><br />However, what did not appeal to me was the objectification of women. I found it rather annoying. <br /><br />Apart from that, this is a lovely collection of elegant and intimate poems.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2144410986026531694" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_31117472" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/31117472" />
    <a title="Ilze" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1147966-ilze"><img alt="Ilze" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1477553914p2/1147966.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/31117472?book_show_action=true">Aug 25, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ilze" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ilze" href="/user/show/1147966-ilze">Ilze</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780224074414" href="/book/show/833091.Twenty_Love_Poems_and_a_Song_of_Despair">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer31117472" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6928213766029342777">Has anyone read and understood the Song of Solomon? Neruda must have. And he must have understood it too! These poems are more than just about the physical love between man and woman: they are about what happens to the soul. For some reason pine trees feature a fair amount here, from " ... as I love you, the pines in the wind / want to sing your name with their leaves of wire" to "I have said that you sang in the wind / like the pines and like the masts. / Like them you are tall and taciturn, / </span>
  <span id="freeText6928213766029342777" style="display:none">Has anyone read and understood the Song of Solomon? Neruda must have. And he must have understood it too! These poems are more than just about the physical love between man and woman: they are about what happens to the soul. For some reason pine trees feature a fair amount here, from " ... as I love you, the pines in the wind / want to sing your name with their leaves of wire" to "I have said that you sang in the wind / like the pines and like the masts. / Like them you are tall and taciturn, / and you are sad, all at once, like a voyage." He wants to "do with you what spring does with the cherry trees", but also knows that "the leaves complain as though they / were sick" in her absence. One can only say that he really loves her (and I wish I could understand the Chilean versions, also printed in the book), or that she left him and his love needed renewing with each new love. My words do not relate his beautiful descriptions for love, so I will be still:<br /><br />I like for you to be still<br />It is as though you are absent<br />And you hear me from far away<br />And my voice does not touch you<br />It seems as though your eyes had flown away<br />And it seems that a kiss had sealed your mouth<br /><br />As all things are filled with my soul<br />You emerge from the things<br />Filled with my soul<br />You are like my soul<br />A butterfly of dream<br />And you are like the word: Melancholy<br /><br />I like for you to be still<br />And you seem far away<br />It sounds as though you are lamenting<br />A butterfly cooing like a dove<br />And you hear me from far away<br />And my voice does not reach you<br />Let me come to be still in your silence<br /><br />And let me talk to you with your silence<br />That is bright as a lamp<br />Simple, as a ring<br />You are like the night<br />With its stillness and constellations<br />Your silence is that of a star<br />As remote and candid<br /><br />I like for you to be still<br />It is as though you are absent<br />Distant and full of sorrow<br />So you would've died<br />One word then, One smile is enough<br />And I'm happy;<br />Happy that it's not true<br /><br />(translated by W.S. Merwin)</span>
  <a data-text-id="6928213766029342777" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer1686150596" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8009625759344141984">The memory of you emerges from the night around me.<br/>The river mingles its stubborn lament with the sea.<br/><br/>Deserted like the wharves at dawn.<br/>It is the hour of departure, oh deserted one! <br/><br/>Cold flower heads are raining over my heart.<br/>Oh pit of debris, fierce cave of the shipwrecked.<br/><br/>In you the wars and the flights accumulated.<br/>From you the wings of the song birds rose.<br/><br/>You swallowed everything, like distance.<br/>Like the sea, like time. In you everything sank!  <br/><br/><br/>Neruda is a magician. Its like he throws his word</span>
  <span id="freeText8009625759344141984" style="display:none">The memory of you emerges from the night around me.<br />The river mingles its stubborn lament with the sea.<br /><br />Deserted like the wharves at dawn.<br />It is the hour of departure, oh deserted one! <br /><br />Cold flower heads are raining over my heart.<br />Oh pit of debris, fierce cave of the shipwrecked.<br /><br />In you the wars and the flights accumulated.<br />From you the wings of the song birds rose.<br /><br />You swallowed everything, like distance.<br />Like the sea, like time. In you everything sank!  <br /><br /><br />Neruda is a magician. Its like he throws his words up in air and they fall back, like rain, endlessly floating around the reader, enchanting him body and soul. His poetry is Beautiful, surreal, haunting and something indescribable, like an ache of a wound long healed. </span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('716UOTmV7bmbOKbDLlC+9/TeOCQWg2E6MUh8mq6xtTfVeP3rgssk0TwTEqLZpbQoIoHf7NgO711qNjorY/JHrQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('UNHYrtU/kwe1M4TR9+qQhu8ci4q9lVBkJmMdPjhbJ7Jq97F8bmFabxIYMLAAH5pZOUNsQnMY3gN9HVuP9RjVKA==')})
  }
//]]>

      <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/4026.Pablo_Neruda">About Pablo Neruda</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/4026.Pablo_Neruda"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1499982432p3/4026.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/4026.Pablo_Neruda">Pablo Neruda
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
7,730
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer11167327889991758775">Pablo Neruda was the pen name and, later, legal name of the Chilean writer and politician Neftalí Ricardo Reyes Basoalto. Neruda assumed his pen name as a teenager, partly because it was in vogue, partly to hide his poetry from his father, a rigid man who wanted his son to have a "practical" occupation. Neruda's pen name was derived from Czech writer and poet Jan Neruda; Pablo is thought to be fro</span>
  <span id="freeText11167327889991758775" style="display:none">Pablo Neruda was the pen name and, later, legal name of the Chilean writer and politician Neftalí Ricardo Reyes Basoalto. Neruda assumed his pen name as a teenager, partly because it was in vogue, partly to hide his poetry from his father, a rigid man who wanted his son to have a "practical" occupation. Neruda's pen name was derived from Czech writer and poet Jan Neruda; Pablo is thought to be from Paul Verlaine. With his works translated into many languages, Pablo Neruda is considered one of the greatest and most influential poets of the 20th century.<br /><br />Neruda was accomplished in a variety of styles, ranging from erotically charged love poems like his collection Twenty Poems of Love and a Song of Despair, surrealist poems, historical epics, and overtly political manifestos. In 1971 Neruda won the Nobel Prize for Literature, a controversial award because of his political activism. Colombian novelist Gabriel García Márquez once called him "the greatest poet of the 20th century in any language."<br /><br />On July 15, 1945, at Pacaembu Stadium in São Paulo, Brazil, he read to 100,000 people in honor of Communist revolutionary leader Luís Carlos Prestes. When Neruda returned to Chile after his Nobel Prize acceptance speech, Salvador Allende invited him to read at the Estadio Nacional before 70,000 people.<br /><br />During his lifetime, Neruda occupied many diplomatic posts and served a stint as a senator for the Chilean Communist Party. When Conservative Chilean President González Videla outlawed communism in Chile, a warrant was issued for Neruda's arrest. Friends hid him for months in a house basement in the Chilean port of Valparaíso. Later, Neruda escaped into exile through a mountain pass near Maihue Lake into Argentina. Years later, Neruda was a close collaborator to socialist President Salvador Allende.<br /><br />Neruda was hospitalized with cancer at the time of the Chilean coup d'état led by Augusto Pinochet. Three days after being hospitalized, Neruda died of heart failure. Already a legend in life, Neruda's death reverberated around the world. Pinochet had denied permission to transform Neruda's funeral into a public event. However, thousands of grieving Chileans disobeyed the curfew and crowded the streets to pay their respects. Neruda's funeral became the first public protest against the Chilean military dictatorship.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11167327889991758775" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
